9997100849804796	$$aI. The language and rhetoric of comedy --Irony lies at the heart of comic technique --All comedy is a manipulation of deceptive appearances --Language is a vast sea of comic possibilities swirling beneath rational discourse --Nonsense, both calculated and spontaneous, is one of the chief by-products of language --Comic aimlessness and stupidity create the illusion of naive, unconscious humor --Popular humor is topical and satirical --Condensation and an epigrammatic style are essential aspects of comic creativity --Perfect timing is the ideal all actors and joke-tellers are striving to achieve --II. Comic characters : convention and types --Conventions are a tacit agreement between playwright and audience about social stereotypes and the ways of representing them --Identity is conventionally defined by external signs, and costume is a significant expression of personality --Comic characters begin with fixed and sometimes arbitrary values and connotations --In most comedies, wit replaces virtue and pretense is the greatest vice --Pretenders are not only deflated in comedy but also displayed and celebrated --Comedy deals not with representative types, but with extremes and caricatures --III. The structure of comedy --Comedies usually begin with a startling pronouncement or an outrageous situation --The comic action is developed by repetition, accumulation, and snowballing --Comedies are expected toe end happily, with feating, dancing, drinking, revelry, and the promise of offspring --IV. Forms of comedy : farce, tragic farce, burlesque, comedy of manners, satire, and festive comedy --A farce is a comedy with an extravagant plot in which anything can happen : the characters are developed by quirks and eccentricities rather than according to any believable, psychological truth --In tragic farce, the themes and techniques of tragedy have been absorbed and comically transformed --Burlesque comedy mocks the moral and stylistic pretensions of tragedy and romance --In comedy of manners the wits are arbiters of good taste, while the butts are awkward, stiff, pretentious, and social misfits --Satiric comedy seeks to display and control its villain-heroes --Festive comedy vanquishes all obstructions and asserts a hedonistic, holiday, carnival spirit --V. Comedy in theory and in practice : seven aspects of the comic hero --Imagines himself to be invulnerable and omnipotent --Indulges in wish fulfillment and fantasy gratification --Engages in play without any ulterior purpose --Is a realist who celebrates the body and affirms the life force --Cultivates comic paranoia, as if laughter were an essential defense against a hostile reality --May serve as the ritual clown of his society, acting as a scapegoat for its taboos --Doesn't eventually merge into the tragic hero, but represents and entirely different range of experience.
